    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="7593b7c1ab7840ee99ee4eaa65bad32d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3a5aef87-82b6-4d59-abde-5bd40144afe9'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xw.ou@siat.ac.cn"><span>xw.ou@siat.ac.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:tangyb@siat.ac.cn"><span>tangyb@siat.ac.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Tang/Yongbing">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d174181013" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-based dual ion batteries (SDIBs) have garnered significant attention as novel energy storage devices offering the advantages of high-voltage and low-cost. Nonetheless, conventional electrolytes exhibit low resistance to oxidation and poor compatibility with electrode materials, resulting in rapid battery failure. In this study, for the first time, a chlorination design of electrolytes for SDIB, is proposed. Using ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC) as a representative, chlorine (Cl)-substituted EMC not only demonstrates increased oxidative stability ascribed to the electron-withdrawing characteristics of chlorine atom, electrolyte compatibility with both the cathode and anode is also greatly improved by forming Cl-containing interface layers. Consequently, a discharge capacity of 104.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> within a voltage range of 3.0–5.0&nbsp;V is achieved for Na||graphite SDIB that employs a high graphite cathode mass loading of 5.0&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, along with almost no capacity decay after 900 cycles. Notably, the Na||graphite SDIB can be revived for an additional 900 cycles through the replacement of a fresh Na anode. As the mass loading of graphite cathode increased to 10&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, Na||graphite SDIB is still capable of sustaining over 700 times with ≈100% capacity retention. These results mark the best outcome among reported SDIBs. This study corroborates the effectiveness of chlorination design in developing high-voltage electrolytes and attaining enduring cycle stability of Na-based energy storage devices.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202402702-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202402702-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) find extensive application in the field of energy storage attributed to their notable energy density and reliable cycling performance. However, the limited resources and uneven distribution of lithium (Li) make it difficult for LIBs to support the applications in both electric vehicles and large-scale energy storage. Therefore, alternative energy storage technologies for LIBs are imperative.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0001_R_d174181005e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Nature">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) hold substantial resource advantages ascribed to the abundant reserves and widespread availability of sodium (Na). However, the sluggish diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> ion caused by its larger ionic radius (1.02 Å vs 0.76 Å of Li<sup>+</sup> ion) within the electrode materials poses a challenge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0002_R_d174181005e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Rev. Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, SIBs generally operate at relatively lower voltages than LIBs originating from the higher redox potential of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (−2.71&nbsp;V vs standard hydrogen electrode, SHE) compared to Li<sup>+</sup>/Li (−3.04&nbsp;V vs SHE),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0003_R_d174181005e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Science Nano Lett. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> limiting the battery energy density. Moreover, cathode materials in SIBs, such as layered oxides, still necessitate the use of transition metals like nickel and manganese, contributing to a large portion of the battery cost.</p>
         
         <p>Emerging as an innovative energy storage device, sodium-based dual ion batteries (SDIBs) offer cost benefits by exploiting graphite as the cathode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0004_R_d174181005e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. J. Phys. Chem. C Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unlike the “rocking-chair” working mechanism of conventional SIBs, SDIBs involve the simultaneous participation of anions and cations in the charge and discharge processes. The graphite cathode uptakes anions at high potentials (&gt;4.2&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), enabling anticipation of high working voltage and energy density for SDIBs. Furthermore, the rapid solvation/desolvation kinetics of anions at the cathode side enhances the rate performance of SDIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0005_R_d174181005e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. ChemSusChem">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, unlike SIBs, serving as both the reservoir and transport medium for active ions, the electrolyte plays a more crucial role in relation to the performance of SDIBs such as anion intercalation capacity, cycle life, and energy density. Nevertheless, conventional electrolyte systems face several challenges in SDIBs: 1) inadequate oxidative stability to match the high-voltage graphite cathode, resulting in unsatisfactory battery capacity and cycle life; 2) poor compatibility with the anode, particularly Na metal, due to its higher reactivity than Li, leading to the unstable electrolyte/anode interface; 3) low electrolyte concentration, causing co-intercalation of solvent molecules and limiting the energy density of SDIBs. For instance, ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC) was a commonly used solvent for lithium-based DIBs (e.g., LiPF<sub>6</sub>/EMC electrolyte) to realize relatively high anion intercalation capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0006_R_d174181005e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, when applied in SDIBs, EMC-based electrolytes exhibited unsatisfactory performance possibly due to the low oxidative stability (graphite cathode in SDIBs was commonly charged to higher potentials than that in Li-based DIBs), insufficient solubility of NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt, and poor compatibility with Na metal anode. Therefore, there is an immediate need to develop highly stable electrolyte systems of favorable electrolyte/electrode compatibility to enhance SDIB performance.</p>
         
         <p>This study presents, for the first time, a chlorination design strategy to develop highly stable electrolytes for SDIBs. As known, traditional carbonate ester-based electrolytes suffer from oxidation under high working potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0007_R_d174181005e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Environ. Sci.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By contrast, due to the electron-withdrawing characteristics of halogen atoms, the introduction of chlorine (Cl) functional group into the carbonate ester solvent molecule can decrease the electron density of carbonyl group, thus lowering the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) energy levels and elevating the oxidation potential. Compared to fluorine (F), Cl atom has weaker electronegativity, thus Cl substitution exerts less influence on solvent polarity and solvation coordination ability. To exemplify this strategy, EMC solvent molecule is selected for chlorination design (i.e., chloromethyl ethyl carbonate, Cl-EMC). Coupled with sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI) salt, a highly stable electrolyte with 4.0 m concentration was developed (4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC). Theoretical calculations and experimental assessments revealed that the HOMO energy level of Cl-EMC was lowered than EMC (from −8.14 to −8.48&nbsp;eV), and consequently, both the anti-oxidative capability of electrolyte and intercalation performance of anion were dramatically improved. Noteworthily, the corrosion of aluminum (Al) current collector was also effectively mitigated using this Cl-EMC and NaFSI-based electrolyte. Meanwhile, at the anode side, the chlorinated electrolyte facilitated the uniform plating and stripping of Na<sup>+</sup>. Further investigations attributed the enhanced performance to the formation of Cl-containing components in the interface layers, significantly improving the electrolyte/electrode compatibility. Utilizing this chlorinated electrolyte (4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC), Na||graphite SDIB achieved a capacity of 104.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and almost no capacity decay over 900 cycles. Notably, after replacing the fresh Na metal anode, the SDIB can be revived, enabling an additional 900 cycles. This research work validates the advantages of chlorination design in developing highly stable electrolytes and provides a new strategy to improve the performance of SDIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202402702-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202402702-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The limited anion intercalation capacity and poor cycling stability of SDIBs with EMC-based electrolyte are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a,S1c</a> (Supporting Information). Although the addition of ethylene carbonate (EC) can assist in dissociating NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt and enhance the stability of electrolyte/anode interface, the discharge capacity (≈80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), cycling stability (≈130 cycles), and Coulombic efficiency (CE) (&lt;90%) of SDIBs were still limited (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b,S1c</a>, Supporting Information). To address these issues, we propose a chlorination design strategy and use EMC as a proof-of-concept to enhance its oxidative stability and electrode compatibility. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402702-fig-0001">1a</a> compares the molecular structure and electrostatic potential diagrams (ESP) of EMC and Cl-EMC solvent molecules. The substitution of an H atom in EMC with a Cl atom resulted in a noticeable decrease in the electron density of the O atom (O in the C = O), confirming the electron-withdrawing nature of the Cl functional group. As a result, Cl-EMC molecule displayed a lower HOMO energy level than EMC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0001">1b</a>), suggesting the improved anti-oxidative capability. Enhanced oxidative stability of Cl-EMC-based electrolytes was verified by linear scanning voltammetry (LSV), using Na metal as the counter electrode and Al as the working electrode. Here, NaFSI was chosen as the electrolyte solute due to its high solubility induced by the large-size anion with weak coordinating properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0008_R_d174181005e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Acc. Chem. Res. Adv. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> LSV results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0001">1c</a>, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) revealed the oxidation potential of 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC significantly surpassing that of 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC and even comparable to 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC. Moreover, increasing the concentration (i.e., 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, also the maximum concentration of NaFSI/Cl-EMC electrolyte) further heightened the oxidation potential of chlorinated electrolyte. The enhanced oxidative stability of chlorinated electrolyte can be also affirmed by comparing the leaking current densities of Na||graphite cells (graphite mass loading: 5.0&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) during the potentiostatic tests hold at 5.0&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), in which 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC displayed a much lower current than that of 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Importantly, compared to NaFSI/EMC, this chlorinated electrolyte demonstrated anti-corrosion property toward the Al current collector, as revealed from the constant voltage measurement results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) and corresponding SEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, despite after chlorination, the ionic conductivity of NaFSI/Cl-EMC electrolyte decreased, 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC still delivered an ionic conductivity of 1.35 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can ensure the efficient ion transport during the charge/discharge process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). It should be noted that, the fluorinated EMC (i.e., 2,2,2-trifluoroethyl methyl carbonate, FEMC) solvent reacted immediately with Na metal, causing the FEMC solution to turn brown-black within 12 h (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the Cl-EMC solution and Na metal surface showed no significant change upon Na metal immersion, highlighting the superior compatibility of Cl-EMC with Na metal over FEMC.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202402702-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c059c64b-0376-4a80-89ca-e54c13efaf5a/adma202402702-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c059c64b-0376-4a80-89ca-e54c13efaf5a/adma202402702-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/65c39aa4-9886-44aa-8633-3aa34b089824/adma202402702-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c059c64b-0376-4a80-89ca-e54c13efaf5a/adma202402702-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402702-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Comparison of molecular structure and ESP of EMC and Cl-EMC; b) Energy level comparison of EMC and Cl-EMC; c) Oxidative stability comparison of different electrolytes (scanning rate: 1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>With enhanced oxidative stability established, we proceed to evaluate the performance of chlorinated electrolyte in Na||graphite SDIBs (mass loading of graphite cathode: 5.0&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>). The cell configuration and schematic diagram of Na||graphite SDIB using the electrolyte with Cl-EMC was displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). As depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402702-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), both the discharge capacity and CE were significantly improved with Cl-EMC-based electrolytes compared to EMC. Specifically, under 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC, the battery discharge capacity was a mere 16.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>; even with 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC, the discharge capacity was still limited (55.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The corresponding CEs were also quite low (29.9% for 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC, 67.2% for 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC). By contrast, 1.0 m and 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC yielded discharge capacities of 47.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 102.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, alongside elevated CE values of 79.4% and 92.8%, respectively. The cyclic voltammetry test results of Na||graphite cells indicated the decreased anion intercalation potential and enhanced redox peak intensity with chlorinated electrolyte, in agreement with the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). During cycling, the discharge capacity can be gradually increased to 104.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, and after that, almost no capacity fade was observed over 900 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0002">2b</a>). By comparison, the battery capacity decayed rapidly and failed after 100 cycles with 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC electrolyte. The CE values of Na||graphite SDIBs under 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC and 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC during cycling are compared in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). With higher mass loading of graphite cathode (10.0&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>), Na||graphite SDIBs still achieved over 700 cycles with 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0002">2c</a> illustrates highly overlapped charge/discharge curves of different cycles under 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, indicating good cycling stability. The electrochemical performance of SDIBs with other NaFSI/Cl-EMC concentrations was presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information). Additionally, during the long cycling test, the median discharge voltage remained stable above 4.3&nbsp;V, in favor of attaining high energy density (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0002">2d</a>). Even at a 4 C current rate, the discharge capacity reached 97.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0002">2e</a>). Na||graphite SDIBs exhibited obvious charge-discharge plateaus and minimal polarization at varying charge-discharge rates, reflecting excellent rate performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Noted that, with the replacement of Na metal after 911 cycles, the Na||graphite SDIB can be revived and enabled another 900 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0002">2f</a>), among the best performance in previously reported SDIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0009_R_d174181005e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources Adv. Energy Mater. Small Nat. Commun. ChemSusChem ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Small Methods ACS Energy Lett. Natl. Sci. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater. Small ChemSusChem">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These findings validate that the chlorinated electrolyte can effectively stabilize the electrolyte/cathode interface and improve anion intercalation performance. Apart from Cl-EMC, the effectiveness of chlorination design toward advanced SDIBs has also been verified by chloroethylene carbonate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). The research on the effect of chlorination degree is our subsequent study, which is underway in our lab.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202402702-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c2af7a56-7fa8-42d2-9abf-8de176519ceb/adma202402702-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c2af7a56-7fa8-42d2-9abf-8de176519ceb/adma202402702-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8ece6e36-55fe-4520-9df9-7c66105219e7/adma202402702-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c2af7a56-7fa8-42d2-9abf-8de176519ceb/adma202402702-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402702-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of Na||graphite SDIBs. a) 10th charge/discharge curves of Na||graphite SDIBs under different electrolytes at 1 C current rate (1 C = 100&nbsp;mA g⁻<sup>1</sup>); (b) Long-term cycling stability test at a current density of 1 C; (c) Corresponding galvanostatic charge-discharge profiles at different cycles; (d) Median discharge voltage during long cycling, inset illustrates the charging/discharging curves for the last 20 cycles; (e) Rate capability; (f) Long-term cycling stability test with the replacement of a fresh Na metal at a current density of 1 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To elucidate the mechanism underlying SDIB performance enhancement with Cl-EMC-based electrolyte, we characterized the morphology, crystallinity, and graphitization degree (G) of graphite cathode before and after cycling by scanning electron microscopy (SEM), X-ray diffraction (XRD), and Raman spectroscopy, respectively. Relative to the pristine graphite electrode (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3a</a>), cycling with 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v) and 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC electrolytes resulted in a rough graphite surface covered with evident accumulations, likely stemming from electrolyte decomposition at high working voltages (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3b,c</a>). By contrast, after cycling in 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, the graphite surface remained smooth and its lamellar structure was retained intact. This indicated the formation of a stable cathode-electrolyte interphase (CEI) layer that effectively curtailed the electrolyte decomposition (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3d</a>). The low magnification SEM images of cycled graphite electrodes are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). XRD results revealed slight left-shifting and broadening of graphite (002) peaks after cycling in 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v) and 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC, whereas 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC preserved the graphite's structural integrity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0010_R_d174181005e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, the calculated interlayer distances of 3.356, 3.364, 3.361, and 3.359 Å were observed for graphite cathode before and after cycling in 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v), 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC, and 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, respectively, corresponding to graphitization degrees of 97.3%, 88.6%, 91.7%, and 94.7% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3g</a>). The enlarged interlayer distance and lowered graphitization degree of graphite after cycling in 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v) and 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC electrolytes can be attributed to solvent molecule co-intercalation or irreversible anion intercalation process, leading to lattice distortion of graphite lamellar structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0011_R_d174181005e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Power Sources Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conversely, the structural integrity of graphite cycled in 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC possibly resulted from the formation of a stable CEI layer that selectively permitted the translocation of anions. Raman spectroscopy showed the peaks at ≈1335 cm<sup>−1</sup> and ≈1575 cm<sup>−1</sup> for D band and G band, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0011_R_d174181005e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Power Sources Adv. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202402702-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0012_R_d174181005e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio, a structural integrity indicator of graphite, was 0.16 for 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, resembling pristine graphite (0.14), yet lower than 0.39 and 0.27 for graphite cycled in 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v) and 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC, respectively. This further certified the effectiveness of Cl-EMC-based electrolyte in maintaining the structural stability of graphite electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3g</a>). Besides, in situ XRD was carried out to explore the anion intercalation behaviors in different electrolytes (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information), and better anion intercalation reversibility and more intact structure of graphite electrode under 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC were observed. Noteworthily, even after 900 cycles, the structural integrity and crystallinity of the graphite electrode were still retained (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202402702-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f695e667-6b93-4575-b12b-8fab90b69e05/adma202402702-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f695e667-6b93-4575-b12b-8fab90b69e05/adma202402702-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4d3a314f-8031-4b6d-8c32-9b07d9c9cef1/adma202402702-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f695e667-6b93-4575-b12b-8fab90b69e05/adma202402702-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402702-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterizations of graphite cathodes. SEM images of pristine graphite before (a) and after 10 cycles in 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v) (b), 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC (c), and 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC (d); e–g) XRD results, Raman spectra, and corresponding graphitization degree as well as I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio of pristine graphite and graphite cathode after cycling in different electrolytes; h–j) High-resolution C 1s, F 1s, and Cl 2p XPS spectra of graphite cathodes after 10 cycles in 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC and NaFSI/Cl-EMC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To examine how chlorinated electrolyte influences the formation of CEI layer, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was conducted to characterize the surface compositions of graphite cathode. Actually, owing to the large volume change of graphite cathode during anion intercalation, the CEI layer should have both good elasticity and mechanical strength.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0013_R_d174181005e778" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison to 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC, after cycling in 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, the intensities of C-O (286.0&nbsp;eV) and C = O (288.5&nbsp;eV) peaks slightly decreased, and an additional peak for C-Cl at 287.2&nbsp;eV emerged (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3h</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0014_R_d174181005e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Joule">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the stronger C-F (688.1&nbsp;eV) peak originated from polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder indicated a thinner CEI layer formed in 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC compared to that in 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3i</a>). Additionally, since the Na-F (684.6&nbsp;eV) component at the cathode side is closely linked to simultaneous solvent molecule dehydrogenation and anion defluorination under high-voltage,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0009_R_d174181005e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources Adv. Energy Mater. Small Nat. Commun. ChemSusChem ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Small Methods ACS Energy Lett. Natl. Sci. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater. Small ChemSusChem">9</a>, <a href="#adma202402702-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0015_R_d174181005e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Phys. Chem. C Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the reduced Na-F ratio in the CEI layer for 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC further implied the suppressed electrolyte decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0016_R_d174181005e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Also, high-resolution XPS of Cl 2p displayed C-Cl (200.5 and 202.1&nbsp;eV) and NaCl (198.4 and 200.0&nbsp;eV) derived from Cl-EMC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0003">3j</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0017_R_d174181005e809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high electronegativity of Cl in C-Cl component can act as the “anchor point”, interacting with the solvent molecules and preventing their co-intercalation into graphite interlayer; the small portion of inorganic components like NaF and NaCl can enhance the mechanical strength of the interface layer as previously reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0018_R_d174181005e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was performed to further investigate the CEI layers. TEM results showed a thinner CEI layer of 5.2&nbsp;nm formed under chlorinated electrolyte, less than that of 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC (14.7&nbsp;nm) and 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC: EMC (1:1 v/v) (24.5&nbsp;nm) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). In summary, chlorinated electrolyte contributed to the formation of a thinner and more stable CEI layer enriched with Cl-containing components under high working voltage. As a comparison, we tested the graphite cycled in 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). The peak intensities of C-O (286.3&nbsp;eV) and C = O (288.5&nbsp;eV) were higher, implying an unstable electrolyte/cathode interface and continuous electrolyte decomposition, aligning with SEM images.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0019_R_d174181005e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Sci.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In addition to graphite cathode, Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping efficiency on the anode also significantly impacts the cycling stability of SDIBs. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402702-fig-0004">4a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information) compare Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping efficiency in different electrolytes using the Aurbach method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0020_R_d174181005e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Mater. ACS Energy Lett. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conventional electrolyte systems such as 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v) and 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC exhibited noticeable hysteresis and failed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0021_R_d174181005e858" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater. InfoMat">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC concentrated electrolyte showed poor Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping reversibility (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). After chlorination design, 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC displayed a Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping CE of 94.0%, a substantial improvement over traditional carbonate ester-based electrolytes, and the CE can be elevated to 96.2% by increasing the chlorinated electrolyte concentration to 4.0 m. Chlorinated electrolyte's advantage in enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping reversibility was also confirmed with Na||Na symmetric cells (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0004">4b</a>). The cell using 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v) could only last less than 100 cycles, with overpotential at 100th cycle nearly tripling compared to 1st cycle (from ±0.14&nbsp;V to ±0.55&nbsp;V), likely linked to an unstable interface and increased interface impedance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0022_R_d174181005e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar unstable Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping was observed for 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). When EMC was replaced by Cl-EMC, much better long-term cycling stability for Na||Na symmetric cells was realized. Specifically, 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC elongated the cycling life from 132 to 280 h compared to 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0004">4b</a>). A more stable voltage curve of small polarization (±0.2&nbsp;V) over 800 h was observed in 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC. With higher areal capacity (0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>), Na||Na symmetric cells can be cycled over 100 h; the relatively larger voltage polarization should be attributed to the high viscosity of 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). These results proved that chlorination design can stabilize the electrolyte/anode interface, allowing the reversible and stable Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202402702-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/78c2cf0f-a30b-4398-b86a-436e408e2f38/adma202402702-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/78c2cf0f-a30b-4398-b86a-436e408e2f38/adma202402702-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f6d83933-0ef6-4996-b43c-d79ece24b025/adma202402702-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/78c2cf0f-a30b-4398-b86a-436e408e2f38/adma202402702-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402702-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Investigation of Na<sup>+</sup> plating/striping behaviors in different electrolytes. a) Aurbach method for Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping test of Na||Cu half cells. Current density: 0.3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, areal density: 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>; b) Cycling stability of Na||Na symmetric cells in different electrolytes, with insets displaying enlarged Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping profiles within selected time ranges; high-resolution C 1s (c–e), F 1s (f–h), and Cl 2p (h–j) XPS spectra of the deposited Na metals from 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC, 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, and 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphology and surface compositions of Na metal deposits under different electrolytes were also compared. As seen from the digital photos and SEM images, Na metal plated from 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1,v:v) and 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC turned out to be uneven and gray-black, while the shiny Na metal can be only found at certain points; besides, Na metal deposits on Cu substrate were either irregular bulks or dendrites (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28a,S28b</a>, Supporting Information). Even with 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC high-concentration electrolyte, Na deposits on Cu substrate were mainly gray-black (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28d</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, under 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC, the deposited Na metal was shiny and uniform, and 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC yielded larger and denser Na metal particles, conducive to reduce exposed surface and side reactions at the interface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28c,S28e</a>, Supporting Information). These digital photos and SEM images clearly indicated more uniform and reversible plating/stripping of Na<sup>+</sup> under chlorinated electrolytes compared to carbonate ester-based electrolytes. Furthermore, we analyzed the solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers formed in different electrolytes by XPS. Unlike 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC, 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC exhibited decreased C-O (285.5&nbsp;eV) peak intensity and a new peak of C-Cl (286.8&nbsp;eV), suggesting the suppressed electrolyte reduction and participation of Cl-EMC in SEI formation process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0004">4c,f</a>). Additionally, high resolution F 1s showed enhanced C-F (688.7&nbsp;eV) and SO<sub>x</sub>-F (687.0&nbsp;eV) components under the chlorinated electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0023_R_d174181005e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As S and F elements exist only in the electrolyte solutes, this implied chlorination design can promote anion involvement in SEI layer formation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0004">4d,g</a>). Moreover, Cl 2p spectra in 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC electrolyte displayed peaks of NaCl (198.4 and 200.0&nbsp;eV) (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0004">4e,h</a>). It was reported that NaCl inorganic component in the SEI layer was beneficial to stabilize the interface and facilitate charge transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0024_R_d174181005e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. JACS Au">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Under 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0004">4i–k</a>), the components of C-Cl and NaCl were enriched, ensuring a more robust SEI layer, and meanwhile, the increased peak intensity of SO<sub>x</sub>-F implied more anions were involved in SEI layer formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0018_R_d174181005e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For comparison, under 1.0 m NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:EMC (1:1, v:v), the SEI layer mainly comprised of organic components and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, reported unfavorable for stable SEI layers (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0025_R_d174181005e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The above results confirm that chlorination design is not only effective in constructing a stable CEI layer, but also improves the reversibility of plating/stripping of Na<sup>+</sup> on the anode. To investigate the fundamental reasons behind the enhancement of chlorinated electrolyte, the electrolyte solvation structure was studied. Raman spectra revealed that the peak intensity at ≈717 cm<sup>−1</sup> (corresponding to S-N-S symmetric stretching vibration) for free FSI<sup>−</sup> anion in 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC was lower than that of 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC, while the peak intensity at ≈731 cm<sup>−1</sup> (corresponding to contact ion pairs, CIPs) was enhanced (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202402702-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0026_R_d174181005e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun. Nat. Commun.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The preference for FSI<sup>−</sup> involvement in the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> was likely due to the reduced binding affinity of Cl-EMC molecule to Na<sup>+</sup>. As a result, more FSI<sup>−</sup> was likely to decompose on the anode, consistent with the stronger SO<sub>x</sub>-F peak intensity in F 1s XPS spectra. In 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC electrolyte, almost no free FSI<sup>−</sup> signal was observed, and the ratio of aggregates (AGGs, ≈744 cm<sup>−1</sup>) was higher than that of 4.0 m NaFSI/EMC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0027_R_d174181005e1040" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the range of 1700–1800 cm<sup>−1</sup>, the blue shift of C = O peak (1758 cm<sup>−1</sup>) in Cl-EMC can be attributed to the electronegative Cl functional group, which weakens the C = O bond (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0028_R_d174181005e1052" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) can directly detect the coordination environment of different electrolyte components. Compared to EMC, H atom that connected to the same carbon as Cl atom showed a downfield shift in the <sup>1</sup>H spectrum, owing to the electron-withdrawing characteristics of Cl, consistent with Raman analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the chemical shift of <sup>23</sup>Na in 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC moved upfield compared to 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC, implying an increased shielding effect, possibly related to the weaker binding strength between solvent molecules and Na<sup>+</sup>, thus enhancing the possibility of FSI<sup>−</sup> into the solvation shell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0005">5b</a>). As the electrolyte concentration increased, <sup>23</sup>Na spectrum further shifted upfield (from −4.3 to −8.5&nbsp;ppm), indicating the enhanced electron density around Na<sup>+</sup>, owing to the increased ratios of CIPs and AGGs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0029_R_d174181005e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Sustainability Res.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The above analysis can also be verified by <sup>19</sup>F NMR. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0005">5c</a>, since FSI<sup>−</sup> anion had a higher chance to enter the solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup> to form CIPs or AGGs in Cl-EMC-based electrolyte, <sup>19</sup>F chemical shift moved to downfield due to the enhanced de-shielding effect of Na<sup>+</sup> on FSI<sup>−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0030_R_d174181005e1099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Energy Lett.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202402702-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0cbfe2a8-1603-4660-9c3c-f9614505c8fd/adma202402702-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0cbfe2a8-1603-4660-9c3c-f9614505c8fd/adma202402702-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8521ff40-adfc-40ac-b100-39caf168aa74/adma202402702-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0cbfe2a8-1603-4660-9c3c-f9614505c8fd/adma202402702-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202402702-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202402702" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Raman spectra comparison of 1.0 and 4.0 m electrolytes with EMC and Cl-EMC solvents; NMR spectra of <sup>23</sup>Na (b) and <sup>19</sup>F (c) in different electrolytes; d) A snapshot of 1.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC electrolyte from AIMD simulation; e,f) Radial distribution functions and coordination numbers of Na<sup>+</sup>-O (EMC), Na<sup>+</sup>-O (FSI<sup>−</sup>), and Na<sup>+</sup>-O (Cl-EMC) pairs in different electrolytes based on AIMD simulations; g) Coordination number comparison in different electrolyte systems; h,i) PDOS of 1.0 m and 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC electrolytes, with insets zooming in the part around LUMO energy.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the solvation structure at the molecular and atomic scale, theoretical simulations were performed. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33a</a> (Supporting Information), the lower binding energy of Na<sup>+</sup>−Cl-EMC (−29.34&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>) than Na<sup>+</sup>−EMC (−33.81&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>) indicated a weaker interaction between Cl-EMC and Na<sup>+</sup>. This explained why FSI<sup>−</sup> was more likely to participate in the solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup>. Additionally, the decreased lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy levels of Na<sup>+</sup>−Cl-EMC complex indicated that the chlorinated solvent was favorable to reduction on the anode, resulting in the formation of Cl-containing components as observed in XPS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33b</a>, Supporting Information). For the anions, the binding energy of FSI<sup>−</sup>−Cl-EMC (−17.4&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>) was calculated to be higher than FSI<sup>−</sup>−EMC (−8.6&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>), suggesting a relatively stronger interaction between Cl-EMC and FSI<sup>−</sup> anion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34a</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0031_R_d174181005e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, FSI<sup>−</sup>−Cl-EMC had a lower HOMO energy level (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34b</a>, Supporting Information), implying that the electrolyte was less prone to oxidate under high voltage. As a result, the byproducts from electrolyte decomposition and Na-F components initiated by the dehydrogenation of solvent molecules on graphite cathode were reduced.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0009_R_d174181005e1188" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources Adv. Energy Mater. Small Nat. Commun. ChemSusChem ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Small Methods ACS Energy Lett. Natl. Sci. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater. Small ChemSusChem">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, ab-initio molecular dynamics simulations (AIMD) were carried out to visualize the coordination environment of different electrolyte systems. The snapshots of electrolyte systems for theoretical simulations are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a> (Supporting Information). As observed from the radial distribution functions and coordination number plots, Na<sup>+</sup> ions mainly coordinated with FSI<sup>−</sup> anions and EMC/Cl-EMC solvents, corresponding to the sharp peaks of Na<sup>+</sup>-O (FSI<sup>−</sup>), Na<sup>+</sup>-O (EMC), and Na<sup>+</sup>-O (Cl-EMC) pairs located at ≈2.4 Å (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0005">5e,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a>, Supporting Information). Compared to 1.0 m NaFSI/EMC, the coordination number ratio of FSI<sup>−</sup> anion to solvent (anion/solvent ratio) for Na<sup>+</sup> increased from 1.99/4.08 to 2.03/2.85, proving that FSI<sup>−</sup> anions tended to enter the solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup> under Cl-EMC solvent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0005">5g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0027_R_d174181005e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a>, <a href="#adma202402702-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0032_R_d174181005e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Energy Storage Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By increasing the electrolyte concentration to 4.0 m, the anion/solvent ratio was further elevated to 2.69/2.93. In addition, the projected density of states (PDOS) verified that in 4.0 m high electrolyte concentration, the LUMO energy of FSI<sup>−</sup> anion was lower than that of Cl-EMC. This implied that FSI<sup>−</sup> anion was more prone to reduce during the SEI formation process, resulting in a SEI layer rich in NaF components, consistent with the XPS results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202402702-fig-0005">5h,i</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202402702-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202402702-bib-0033_R_d174181005e1244" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Energy">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Noted that, based on experimental tests and theoretical simulations, despite the electronegativity of Cl atom, Na<sup>+</sup> mainly interacts with O of C = O group in this work.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202402702-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202402702-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In this study, we put forward a chlorination design aimed at developing high-voltage electrolytes and improve the performance of SDIBs. As a proof-of-concept, the chlorinated electrolyte based on Cl-EMC displayed notably enhanced oxidative stability and better compatibility with the electrodes. It has been demonstrated that 4.0 m NaFSI/Cl-EMC electrolyte yielded exceptional cycling stability (&gt;900 cycles) of Na||graphite SDIB with a high mass loading of graphite cathode (5.0&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>), along with negligible capacity fade and discharge median voltage exceeding 4.3&nbsp;V. Besides, by replacing Na metal, the SDIB can be revived for another 900 charge/discharge cycles. Experimental results and theoretical calculations revealed that the chlorination design can not only strengthen the anti-oxidative capability of solvent molecules, but also help form a stable CEI layer on the graphite cathode, which effectively inhibited electrolyte decomposition at high voltage and preserved the structural integrity of graphite cathode throughout the cycling process. Furthermore, the chlorinated electrolyte facilitated the uniform and efficient plating and stripping of Na<sup>+</sup> ions at the anode side by forming the SEI layer abundant in components like NaCl and NaF. This study showcases the efficacy of chlorination design in developing highly stable electrolytes and introduces a new strategy for the enhancement of SDIB performance.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
